          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1028219426" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12634859451740849762">I saw this movie years ago. Loved it. Still love it. Top ten all-time favorites list. And I didn't know it was based on a book until a few years ago, but it automatically went on my TBR. Because it was going to be just as amazing.<br/><br/>Needless to say, it's difficult for me to extricate one from the other here. It's also difficult for me to explain the book to anyone who hasn't read it, because I'm next-to-positive most of us have seen the movie (if not, you may want to pick up the book first in this </span>
  <span id="freeText12634859451740849762" style="display:none">I saw this movie years ago. Loved it. Still love it. Top ten all-time favorites list. And I didn't know it was based on a book until a few years ago, but it automatically went on my TBR. Because it was going to be just as amazing.<br /><br />Needless to say, it's difficult for me to extricate one from the other here. It's also difficult for me to explain the book to anyone who hasn't read it, because I'm next-to-positive most of us have seen the movie (if not, you may want to pick up the book first in this instance). I can say the book is mostly written in epistolary format and, comparatively, the twists aren't necessarily the same. In both cases, each version of this story is such an incredible mindfuck that I'm sitting here an hour after reading the final ten pages still going WTF WAS THATTTTTT.<br /><br />PS the audio is wonderful if you're an audiobook person. I'm off to rewatch the glorious ambiguity that is the Christopher Nolan movie and see if I can't pick up more connections now that I've read this lovely beast.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12634859451740849762" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_246315418" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/246315418" />
    <a title="Becky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1376766-becky"><img alt="Becky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582132250p2/1376766.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/246315418?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/1376766-becky">Becky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=multi-dimensional">multi-dimensional</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=mystery-and-thrillers">mystery-and-thrillers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=romance-y">romance-y</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer246315418" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3872122635540337483">3.5 Stars <br/><br/>This was... Hmm. This book was not anything I expected. Though, I'm not really sure just <i>what</i> I expected, to be honest. I alternated between thinking that this story was going to be dry and boring, or over the top "magicky", or all fluffadelic like what I expect The Night Circus would be like if I could bring myself to read it. I don't like circuses, or... well, parties or performances or celebrations or fairs or festivals or whatever authors write about to make their books "lively" or</span>
  <span id="freeText3872122635540337483" style="display:none">3.5 Stars <br /><br />This was... Hmm. This book was not anything I expected. Though, I'm not really sure just <i>what</i> I expected, to be honest. I alternated between thinking that this story was going to be dry and boring, or over the top "magicky", or all fluffadelic like what I expect The Night Circus would be like if I could bring myself to read it. I don't like circuses, or... well, parties or performances or celebrations or fairs or festivals or whatever authors write about to make their books "lively" or something. They just don't interest me, and I zone out and then when I next have a conscious thought, I have a string of drool inching its way down my face and realize I've misplaced 20 minutes of my life. Which is another thing that I thought would work against this one for me. But surprisingly it didn't. <br /><br />I think that this one worked because of the realism. This wasn't "magic", this was "illusion". Illusion has rules, and relies on real life physics and ability, rather than just "Oh, I'm going to imagine something and then POOF! it will appear from thin air using only the power of my mind and magic." Yaaa-*conk-snore*<br /><br />As I've recently been reading more fantasy that deals with magic lately, I've come to realize that clearly set rules and laws of magic are vital for me to enjoy a story containing it. Jim Butcher's Dresden Files series works for me for that very reason (among others) - he's created a system of magic that not only makes sense, but has limitations and boundaries and laws. Yes, creativity and imagination shape the outcome of such magic, but only in the desired effect. There are limitations to what can be done, and how, and there's a cost for it. If you want to use force, that force comes from somewhere that will suffer a lack until the balance is restored. Nothing just appears from nothing. <br /><br />So, ramble aside, the fact that this was indeed NOT magic of the fantasy sense but rather magic of the stage illusionist sense allowed me to enjoy the story much more than I thought I would, despite the fact that many would probably feel that illusion is boring compared to magic. <br /><br />I didn't think so, though. I thought it was interesting and well written, though, admittedly some sections were dry and overly detailed for the little that was actually conveyed. I enjoyed the feud between the two illusionists, and enjoyed seeing the events from both perspectives. When things started really getting interesting around the 2nd half, I was thrilled because things that had confused me in the 1st half were now starting to click and make sense. It was Celine Dion was crooning, "It's All Coming Back To Me Now"... <br /><br />I really enjoyed the inclusion of Nikola Tesla, and was happy to see him given the credit he deserved as a mothereffing genius. Because he was. Tesla &gt; Edison. (PS. <a href="http://theoatmeal.com/comics/tesla" rel="nofollow noopener">The Oatmeal. Read it.</a>) <br /><br />Anywho, the apparatus was... fascinating. Possible? No idea. I get confused subtracting double digit numbers (Do I carry the one? Remainder? Multiply by pi? Should I have all these extra parts? Why are these instructions all in Korean??! It's hopeless, really.) so physics, and energy, and matter transference are light years times infinity beyond me. <br /><br />I liked the mysteriousness of the story, but I would have liked a little more closure and definition at the end. But still, very good overall, and I quite enjoyed it, and Simon Vance's reading, which can sometimes be hit or miss for me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3872122635540337483" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_63933798" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63933798" />
    <a title="SR" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/272092-sr"><img alt="SR" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522379704p2/272092.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63933798?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="SR" class="user" itemprop="url" name="SR" href="/user/show/272092-sr">SR</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272092-sr?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272092-sr?shelf=epistolary">epistolary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272092-sr?shelf=narrative-quantum">narrative-quantum</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63933798" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2739598130713003016">page 290: "It is difficult to deal with massy, inorganic compounds. Living tissue is not of the same order of problem."<br/><br/>page 291: "...it would be a simple matter with life organisms. The structure is so much simpler than that of the elements."<br/><br/>...WHAT.<br/><br/>I threw the book (at a couch, and when it bounced and landed open I rescued it immediately). What the hell. And at the same time blithely saying that "energy and matter are but two manifestations of the same force" in 1900 (mass-energy equivalence wa</span>
  <span id="freeText2739598130713003016" style="display:none">page 290: "It is difficult to deal with massy, inorganic compounds. Living tissue is not of the same order of problem."<br /><br />page 291: "...it would be a simple matter with life organisms. The structure is so much simpler than that of the elements."<br /><br />...WHAT.<br /><br />I threw the book (at a couch, and when it bounced and landed open I rescued it immediately). What the hell. And at the same time blithely saying that "energy and matter are but two manifestations of the same force" in 1900 (mass-energy equivalence was first proposed in a 1905 paper by Einstein) - NO. You do not get to anachronistically rock E=MC^2 while simultaneously saying that <i>metals</i> are more complicated than <i>people</i>.<br /><br /><br />End: ...Oh, what the hell was that.<br /><br /><br /><b>12/19/2016</b><br />Someone requested that I cut the book slack on the scientific failure central to the narrative - in, from what I remember having read seven years ago, a story where the protagonist insisted upon scientifically accurate underpinnings for all of his performance bits - because it is fiction. Sure, it's fiction, but the characters themselves voiced valuation of scientific accuracy above all. By 1900, the existence of atomic ratios in metals, alloys, and metallic oxides had been in play for nearly a century. Avogadro's main discoveries of ratios were reported in 1811, based on tin oxide. Bravais' work on crystalline lattices, observed <i>only</i> in metals, oxides, and minerals, was performed in 1850. JJ Thomson discovered electrons in 1897, three years before the setting of the book.<br /><br />This was in the age of scientific showmanship - see Tesla, the importance of AC lighting at the Chicago World's Fair of 1893, Edison's flashy (literally) exhibits, the ongoing obsession with telegraphy and radio - and Thomson's focus on metals in the "third Maxwell volume" would not have been unnoticed, even by lay readers. It's inconceivable to think that science-obsessed performers - let alone scientists themselves - would still hold this animistic view of materials, where the living principle is seen as a simplifying characteristic rather than an emergent effect of compounding complexities, in 1900. Any modern author who wishes to write otherwise is either terribly confused or wilfully messing around, and the failure on Priest's part to reconcile his fictional characters with their historical setting broke the book for me.<br /><br />In addition, it was poorly written and relied too much on would-be atmospheric tension, while the protagonist was a hateful small-minded ball of failure.<br /><br />So, no, no slack will be cut. Forsaking the actual understanding of the history of scientific discovery for the sake of the excitement of writing about an experiment on living cats is flat laziness. Look, I can fix it in a paragraph: Kill the sentence on page 290 and modify that around 291 to "The elements and their alloys and oxides proved too simplistic for satisfaction, a child's trick of magnetic repulsion at a distance. Experimentation progressed rapidly to life organisms, in all their emergent complexity and seeming simplicity of form."<br /><br />(That still leaves the problem of how execrably the novel was written. I can't fix that in a paragraph.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="2739598130713003016" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_893375257" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/893375257" />
    <a title="Eilonwy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17237503-eilonwy"><img alt="Eilonwy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506803497p2/17237503.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/893375257?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eilonwy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eilonwy" href="/user/show/17237503-eilonwy">Eilonwy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312147051" href="/book/show/1691304.The_Prestige">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17237503-eilonwy?shelf=frustrating">frustrating</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17237503-eilonwy?shelf=beautifully-written">beautifully-written</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17237503-eilonwy?shelf=fast-paced">fast-paced</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17237503-eilonwy?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17237503-eilonwy?shelf=what-was-even-the-point">what-was-even-the-point</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer893375257" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10798666433464092035"><blockquote>In 1998, a young woman lures a reporter to her family’s estate, where she tells him they are both descended from two stage magicians who carried on a feud for their entire careers from 1878 through 1903 -- and which she believes is still affecting the current generation. She first provides the young man with a memoir written by his great-grandfather, then tells him her memories of a childhood incident which directly involved both of them, and finally gives him her own great-grandfather’s account</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText10798666433464092035" style="display:none"><blockquote>In 1998, a young woman lures a reporter to her family’s estate, where she tells him they are both descended from two stage magicians who carried on a feud for their entire careers from 1878 through 1903 -- and which she believes is still affecting the current generation. She first provides the young man with a memoir written by his great-grandfather, then tells him her memories of a childhood incident which directly involved both of them, and finally gives him her own great-grandfather’s account of the professional rivalry. The stories solve a mystery the young man has wondered about all his life … but leaves readers with lots, lots more questions than answers. </blockquote>I was madly in love with this book for most of the first three quarters of the story. The modern framing was fascinating, as it sets up a very intriguing question about Andrew Westerly, born Nicholas Borden, that he has never been able to answer, and introduces Kate Angier, who appears to have the information he lacks. Alfred Borden’s reminiscences held my attention despite his rather bloated ego, as his talk about staging magic tricks was interesting, and the contributions of “I and I” (or should I say “I &amp; I”?) were well paced and kept me guessing. Kate Angier’s story about the childhood incident was absolutely chilling, and from thereon I was riveted through Rupert Angier’s diary, as Alfred’s secrets appeared likely to be revealed and to come to a terrifying head with Kate’s memories. <br /><br />But then … oh, then. Why oh why mess up this fantastic work of fiction by introducing a real-world character in the person of Nikola Tesla, having him create a custom invention/discovery which does not exist in real life, and then letting him just wander out of the book without crowing about his achievement or ever reproducing it…?!?! This was a terribly jarring note for me, and a misstep from which the book never recovered. <br /><br />Then the truth about Alfred Borden is revealed, and it’s both boring and not very believable. <br /><br />And after that, the truth about Rupert Angier and Nicholas Borden/Andrew Westerly is revealed, and it’s completely unbelievable, and left me with far, far too many questions, none of which have satisfactory possible answers. Plus the ending is very gothic, but far too open for my tastes. Perhaps it’s fitting that a book about stage magicians should end on a note of massive hand-waving by the author, but I refuse to suspend disbelief or look away from the massive plot holes that finish this book. <br /><br />Five stars for most of this book, which kept me glued to the story and gave me a huge reading hangover. One star for that final quarter, which just went wrong. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10798666433464092035" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_182181184" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/182181184" />
    <a title="Wastrel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5743748-wastrel"><img alt="Wastrel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1317660831p2/5743748.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/182181184?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wastrel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wastrel" href="/user/show/5743748-wastrel">Wastrel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780575075801" href="/book/show/1079782.The_Prestige">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone who wants to see a masterful writer at work and who doesn't mind a bit of weirdness</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5743748-wastrel?shelf=z-2010">z-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5743748-wastrel?shelf=brilliant">brilliant</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5743748-wastrel?shelf=fantastika-liminalia">fantastika-liminalia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer182181184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9415012684152010848">Not recommended for: anyone allergic to SF-elements or who demands a simple story told from beginning to end, or who can't cope with old-fashioned prose or a slow pace.<br/><br/>This book is quite simply a masterwork. From a slow beginning, it ratchets up the tension like an old-fashioned horror film, until it's truly thrilling. With relatively little in the way of overt psychological insight - particularly into Borden, one of the two main characters - it nonetheless constructs clear, sympathetic, underst</span>
  <span id="freeText9415012684152010848" style="display:none">Not recommended for: anyone allergic to SF-elements or who demands a simple story told from beginning to end, or who can't cope with old-fashioned prose or a slow pace.<br /><br />This book is quite simply a masterwork. From a slow beginning, it ratchets up the tension like an old-fashioned horror film, until it's truly thrilling. With relatively little in the way of overt psychological insight - particularly into Borden, one of the two main characters - it nonetheless constructs clear, sympathetic, understandable, totally human protagonists. While clearly enthralled by its own concepts and conceits and determined to play around in the areas marked 'post-modern' and 'literary', it is at the same time able to stand proudly as a totally readable, even old-fashioned, almost Victorian, mystery-thriller. Not that much really happens by blockbuster standards, and huge sections of prose are descriptions of entirely visual things - but these set-piece descriptions are spectacular and vivid, and frankly more visually compelling than any cinematic interpretation could be.<br /><br />Underpinning all this success is Priest's prose, which may not scream for attention every paragraph but which is nonetheless quietly brilliant - I'm tempted to say virtually perfect. Most of the novel is in the form of two diary extracts by different men, each giving their experiences of the same events - both of them are middle-aged, English, Victorian stage magicians, and yet Priest manages to convey two totally characters simply through fine distinctions in narrative voice, the personality of the two writers dripping from every word and every idiosyncracy of syntax. This is a novel that should be taught in schools - not just because of the many, many themes and notions it introduces, but simply as an example of how to write.<br /><br />It's also got one of the biggest narrative twist-kicks I've ever seen - as in I read one particular line and suddenly needed to re-read the last thirty pages while saying 'woah.....' a lot. You really won't be expecting it. And no, it's not one of the twists that makes it to the film. It's twistier than that.<br /><br />That said, it's not perfect. I felt a little emotionally detached throughout, never quite completely involved, though I couldn't help myself turning the pages. The framing story isn't given enough attention, and feels weak compared to the two central narratives; the ending is by itself superb, but feels a bit unconnected to the rest of the story. Sometimes it feels that he should let himself go down a fascinating diversion, rather than force himself to hurry along with the plot. There are some other minor quibbles I could make as well. But they really would be minor.<br /><br />It is, by the way, better than the (still quite good) film - it's deeper, it's cleverer, it's less simplistic, it's more exciting, less predictable, and it's more suffused with class and quality. However, don't come to this from the film assuming you'll love it - it's very different in tone, and with it's old-fashioned (though more readable than genuine Victorian literature!) style, it's slower, and may be less immediately accessible (but stick with it). On the other hand, don't assume there's no point reading this if you've seen the film, as there's a lot more to this than there is to the film, and the film doesn't really spoil the book very much.<br />[I read the book first]<br /><br /><br /><a href="http://vacuouswastrel.wordpress.com/2010/03/20/reaction-the-prestige-christopher-priest/" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read a more substantial review of the novel here.</a> </span>
  <a data-text-id="9415012684152010848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer733391654" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3338586499606280727">I wish I could award half stars, for this book truly would be 3.5 stars--better that “I liked it” but less than “I really liked it.” I was engaged while I was reading, but every time I set it down, I had a struggle to pick it back up again. Totally on me, it’s not the book.<br/><br/>If you enjoyed Robertson Davies’ <i>Deptford Trilogy</i> (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/74406.Fifth_Business__The_Deptford_Trilogy___1_' title='Fifth Business (The Deptford Trilogy, #1) by Robertson Davies' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fifth Business</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/114496.The_Manticore__The_Deptford_Trilogy___2_' title='The Manticore (The Deptford Trilogy, #2) by Robertson Davies' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Manticore</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41098.World_of_Wonders__The_Deptford_Trilogy___3_' title='World of Wonders (The Deptford Trilogy, #3) by Robertson Davies' rel='nofollow noopener'>World of Wonders</a>), you will probably enjoy this book too. Unlike Davies, the ending felt rather <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35031085.Frankenstein' title='Frankenstein by Mary Wollstonecraft Shelley' rel='nofollow noopener'>Frankenstein</a>-like to me. And I have to wonder if</span>
  <span id="freeText3338586499606280727" style="display:none">I wish I could award half stars, for this book truly would be 3.5 stars--better that “I liked it” but less than “I really liked it.” I was engaged while I was reading, but every time I set it down, I had a struggle to pick it back up again. Totally on me, it’s not the book.<br /><br />If you enjoyed Robertson Davies’ <i>Deptford Trilogy</i> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/74406.Fifth_Business__The_Deptford_Trilogy___1_" title="Fifth Business (The Deptford Trilogy, #1) by Robertson Davies" rel="nofollow noopener">Fifth Business</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/114496.The_Manticore__The_Deptford_Trilogy___2_" title="The Manticore (The Deptford Trilogy, #2) by Robertson Davies" rel="nofollow noopener">The Manticore</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41098.World_of_Wonders__The_Deptford_Trilogy___3_" title="World of Wonders (The Deptford Trilogy, #3) by Robertson Davies" rel="nofollow noopener">World of Wonders</a>), you will probably enjoy this book too. Unlike Davies, the ending felt rather <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35031085.Frankenstein" title="Frankenstein by Mary Wollstonecraft Shelley" rel="nofollow noopener">Frankenstein</a>-like to me. And I have to wonder if Erin Morgenstern read this before she wrote <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9361589.The_Night_Circus" title="The Night Circus by Erin Morgenstern" rel="nofollow noopener">The Night Circus</a>. I also keep thinking about Faust for some reason that I can’t put my finger on.<br /><br />I’ve run into Nikola Tesla as a character in fiction on a number of occasions now, and here he is again! I can see the appeal--an extremely intelligent and talented man, but eccentric and (at least in younger years) darkly handsome. <br /><br />If you’re not a fan of the epistolary format, you may want to give this book a miss. But if you love the idea of dueling magicians, this is the book for you.<br /><br /><b>Book number 350 in my Science Fiction and Fantasy reading project.</b><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3338586499606280727" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_420913502" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/420913502" />
    <a title="Arielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6762949-arielle"><img alt="Arielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452561487p2/6762949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/420913502?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arielle" href="/user/show/6762949-arielle">Arielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6762949-arielle?shelf=fantasy-unusual">fantasy-unusual</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6762949-arielle?shelf=everyday-magic">everyday-magic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer420913502" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15913262400574267572">Having loved the film (but also been somewhat bewildered by it - never watch intricate films when you should be sleeping - they really won't make sense.) I was pretty excited to find that it was originally a book. This, I figured, would be my chance to actually understand the story!<br/><br/>Well, sort of.<br/><br/>The book is written in epistolary form, from the point of view of four main characters around 5 generations apart. Two characters write in diaries - these are the main characters, and the storyline that </span>
  <span id="freeText15913262400574267572" style="display:none">Having loved the film (but also been somewhat bewildered by it - never watch intricate films when you should be sleeping - they really won't make sense.) I was pretty excited to find that it was originally a book. This, I figured, would be my chance to actually understand the story!<br /><br />Well, sort of.<br /><br />The book is written in epistolary form, from the point of view of four main characters around 5 generations apart. Two characters write in diaries - these are the main characters, and the storyline that the film is based on. To be honest, I saw very little point in the inclusion of the younger two characters, set in our time and seeking to unravel the mystery of their ancestors. I think their parts added a little suspense as more and more secrets were revealed, slowly.<br /><br />The writing style was very different from what I had expected, but I found it very successful. It made the characters unlikeable and yet also somehow relatable, and I ended up genuinely caring for them, to my surprise. <br /><br />There are a huge number of plot twists, though some are so foreshadowed that it is hardly a revelation when they are revealed at last - more of a "I <i>knew</i> I was right!" moment. <br /><br />I wish I had read the book before seeing the film - the film would have made more sense and, in turn, the book would have retained its secrets for longer, I think. However I can definitely recommend discovering both forms of this story - it's a good one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15913262400574267572" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2124405530" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8368290420465308852">I was first introduced to the book by seeing the movie. I found the movie to be creative and did not see the twist ending coming. I was thrilled that this was a book, and put on the long (long, long, God it's so long) TBR list. And then promptly forgot it.<br/><br/>Fast forward to 2017, and I stumble across a copy of the story. It was time to read the book and remove at least one more book from my TBR list.<br/><br/>Because I <u>had</u> seen the movie, the twist in the story was not the surprise it should have been. That </span>
  <span id="freeText8368290420465308852" style="display:none">I was first introduced to the book by seeing the movie. I found the movie to be creative and did not see the twist ending coming. I was thrilled that this was a book, and put on the long (long, long, God it's so long) TBR list. And then promptly forgot it.<br /><br />Fast forward to 2017, and I stumble across a copy of the story. It was time to read the book and remove at least one more book from my TBR list.<br /><br />Because I <u>had</u> seen the movie, the twist in the story was not the surprise it should have been. That was ok though, because the movie ended at the twist but the book has So. Much. More.<br /><br />I get to say something about this book I don't think I have ever said in a review. The book and the movie are both great, even though they are essentially radically different. Read the book. Watch the movie. Hell, do both. You won't be disappointed.<br /><br /><br /></span>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Illusionists, puzzlers, hucksters</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26876584-joe-valdez?shelf=sci-fi-general">sci-fi-general</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer914856701&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer914856701" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9267237519935845954">For two-thirds of its length, <i>The Prestige</i> is a six-star novel out of five possible stars, an elaborate and beautifully rendered tale of dueling magicians in turn of the century London. The smoke and mirrors of stagecraft and the intrigue of two duplicitous men who take their professional grudge to the grave has the makings of a great novel by itself, but unfortunately and almost fatally, an element of science fiction is introduced in the third act that sends the book off the rails. <br/><br/>Christopher </span>
  <span id="freeText9267237519935845954" style="display:none">For two-thirds of its length, <i>The Prestige</i> is a six-star novel out of five possible stars, an elaborate and beautifully rendered tale of dueling magicians in turn of the century London. The smoke and mirrors of stagecraft and the intrigue of two duplicitous men who take their professional grudge to the grave has the makings of a great novel by itself, but unfortunately and almost fatally, an element of science fiction is introduced in the third act that sends the book off the rails. <br /><br />Christopher Priest begins his tale in the present day, when Andrew Westley, a young journalist with some experience in the occult, is summoned to the village of Caldlow with reports of a Christian cult leader appearing before his followers while locked in a California prison at the same time. Westley -- an orphan haunted by the sense that he is a twin, in spite of birth records which negate that -- is the ancestor of London stage magician Alfred Borden. Soon after meeting with the young Lady Katherine Angier, Westley discovers she's descendent of his ancestor's bitter enemy, magician Rupert Angier. This means nothing to Westley, who'd rather forget the father who gave him up for adoption, but Kate informs Westley that they've met before, when Westley was three years old, in her house, on a night that has haunted her for many years. <br /><br />The novel moves back to the turn of the century, with the journals of Alfred Borden, son of a master wheelwright who ascends to heights of a professional magician, where a knowledge of woodwork comes in handy in his stage career. Borden seeks to expose the spiritists profiting off of gullible Londoners with seances cloaked in standard magic techniques. One of these seances is led by magician Rupert Angier, an industry troll whose performance with his wife and assistant Julia Fensell is exposed as a fraud by Borden. Angier retaliates by heckling or sabotaging Borden's acts for years. Borden sees an opportunity for revenge when his new assistant Olivia Wenscombe reveals that she was initially sent by Angier to spy on Borden before falling in love with her employer. Olivia tantalizes Borden with the secrets of In A Flash, his foe's astonishing signature act.<br /><br />Rupert Angier's version is quite different. Son to the Earl of Colderdale and heir to an estate in Caldlow, Angier scraps a living as a magician by writing to trade publications and making a name for himself as an industry expert. His work as a spiritist provides a steady income, as well as a service for grieving families, until a seance is sabotaged by a rival magician named Alfred Borden. The disruption injures Angier's assistant and wife Julia, terminating her pregnancy. Angier vows revenge, particularly after Borden sabotages a water cabinet and almost drowns Angier on stage. The magician dispatches his assistant and lover Olivia Wenscombe to spy on Borden and learn the secrets of The New Transported Man, his foe's astonishing signature act. Both acts have obvious secrets which anyone, from rival magicians to audiences, suspects they know, but would probably rather not know.<br /><br />In print, <i>The Prestige</i> has the strengths and weaknesses of the 2006 film version directed by Christopher Nolan and adapted by Nolan with his brother Jonathan. The movie omitted the present day wraparound story but retains the fascinating rivalry between two men who engage in subterfuge for a living, an area that the Nolans obviously know a lot about. The book and the movie run off the rails at the 2/3's mark by branching off from the stage feud to send Angier on a trek to Colorado Springs, where Nikola Tesla is experimenting with electricity. Inserting a historical figure into what had been a riveting and imaginative "behind the scenes" tale of deception brings the story to a halt, while hair brained liberties taken with Tesla's work and a stupid science fiction element introduced unravels the story.<br /><br />Priest was always going to run into a problem generating mystique Angier and Borden's "amazing" acts, only to reveal they were executed using very mundane techniques. Priest acknowledges the disappointment that results from peeking behind the curtain, using wit and terrific attention to detail to brace the reader for getting answers to questions they really don't want to know. He explores the pact between entertainer and audience, and how the deceit of the entertainer can consume his life long after the show's over. But in trying to offer the reader an otherworldly development where he didn't need to, the credibility of the story is shot. I almost refuse to acknowledge the last third of the book exists and instead, absorb the trickery and tragedy of the duel between the magicians. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9267237519935845954" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_15238129" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15238129" />
    <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes"><img alt="Nancy Oakes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507555979p2/900340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15238129?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy Oakes" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes">Nancy Oakes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312147051" href="/book/show/1691304.The_Prestige">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer15238129" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10577392011573746968"> I must say that this was a sterling novel that absolutely kept me reading until the very end. But before I launch into it, here's my recommendation. The book thoroughly requires the reader's participation. It's not a wham-bam thank you ma'am kind of story and definitely NOT for readers who quickly become impatient with what they're reading. It is one of those books where you're going to be thinking about what's going constantly as you read, so don't pick it up if you think you're just going to </span>
  <span id="freeText10577392011573746968" style="display:none"> I must say that this was a sterling novel that absolutely kept me reading until the very end. But before I launch into it, here's my recommendation. The book thoroughly requires the reader's participation. It's not a wham-bam thank you ma'am kind of story and definitely NOT for readers who quickly become impatient with what they're reading. It is one of those books where you're going to be thinking about what's going constantly as you read, so don't pick it up if you think you're just going to buzz through this one. You're not.<br /><br />I cannot go into too much detail because it will give away the story. The novel begins with a young news reporter who senses that somewhere deep in his past he had a twin, and even though he's checked birth records (he was adopted at age 3), he finds no trace of a sibling. Yet, he has that sort of telepathy unique to twins, and so he must keep searching. However, that's just a brief moment in the story...the real action takes place between a 19th-century magician named Alfred Borden who, for many reasons which I won't explain here, has a lifelong rivalry with another magician of the period, Rupert Angier. Their story is told through their respective diaries, and it's not until you've finished that you realize just what an excellent job Christopher Priest has done framing this extraordinary tale. He has done it much in the way a magician performs a spectacular illusion, where you, the reader, are the viewer of the trick, and it's not until it's over that you feel the effects (in the parlance of magicians, the "prestige" of what you've just read. I can't remember anything else quite like this in the recent past.<br /><br />I very highly recommend this novel with one major caveat. The end is so abrupt, I was looking to make sure pages hadn't been torn out of my book. However, the rest of the book is so incredible that it will make up for the strange ending. VERY HIGHLY recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10577392011573746968" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_41542'>

</a>

    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {

      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jq1wKnOHUcsaY07IgNrPuIx8vT2jUfMqgNtvr/E7q/HuGpBOoRO1DjyRhSdIxPWV80i4HMcgyxZSp4yeMNVWdA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('D3puNkxVkiy1BjFKwGY39fzwmWThDElyJfXxsCD51bFvzY5SnsF26ZP0+qUIeA3Yg8ScRYV9cU73iRKB4RcoNA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Y eso se conoce como \'proceso de investigación\'. Es donde el autor se da cuenta de si una historia va a merecer o no la pena ser contada. Ahí es donde surgen los verda<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1856386974646591453\" style=\"display:none\">La fase más crítica de un escritor es la fase previa a la elaboración de una obra. Y eso se conoce como \'proceso de investigación\'. Es donde el autor se da cuenta de si una historia va a merecer o no la pena ser contada. Ahí es donde surgen los verdaderos dilemas, los potenciales problemas y innumerables quebraderos de cabeza. Y ahí es, precisamente, donde esta historia inicia su andadura...\n\nMarta Marferny es escritora. Se toma muy a pecho su trabajo y, fiel a sus principios, indaga sin descanso para que sus lectores se sientan cómodos con el trabajo llevado a cabo. Es durante esa indagación cuando, casi de improviso, topa con un antiguo documento. Nada indica que se trate de algo más que un viejo legado, algo que pasará sin pena ni gloria como un simple apoyo para su nueva novela. Sin embargo, Marferny sospecha poco que aquel documento será el principio de una aventura, mayor y más compleja que ninguna de las que su mente haya podido siquiera imaginar. Porque ahora ella se convierte en protagonista de una historia sin final decidido. Aquel viejo documento cambiará no sólo su vida, sino la de miles de personas que pueden verse afectadas por lo que en allí está escrito. \n\nPero, ¿qué misterio se enconde en un viejo documento que puede cambiar el curso de tantas vidas? La respuesta es sencilla. En aquellos legajos se desvelará la existencia de una secta, aparentemente secreta y desconocida que responde al nombre de La Orden griega. Se erige como guardiana del pergamino, y de sus secretos, y ahora Marta está no sólo al descubierto, sino aparentemente involucrada en la supuesta misión que la secta cree estar destinada a desempeñar.\n\nPero Marta no estará sola. Atreverse a no contar con ayuda supondría un resultado de la historia nada alentador para ella. Afortunadamente su mejor amigo, Alexandros, estará a su lado en el camino de la trama. Juntos seguirán las pistas que los grandes maestres de la secta, a lo largo del tiempo y de la Historia, dejaron atrás. Sólo hay que saber buscar y, sobre todo, interpretar. Ahí radica su éxito, y ahí radica su propia seguridad. Puesto que el gran secreto que se esconde tras esta aventura es conocer a no sólo a una especie legendaria, sino conocer en cierta medida el camino que la propia humanidad ha seguido en su desarrollo.\n\nEl protocolo griego es un thriller que entremezcla misterio, historia, aventura y, según el momento y su contexto, un cierto halo de terror light que encaja a la perfección dentro de la trama. La cuidada puesta en escena de los personajes, y los cuidados escenarios que el autor, Kendall Maison, ha elegido para el desarrollo de la obra, hacen que el lector se vea inmerso en una historia por países tan dispares como Turquía, Egipto o España, y por tramos de la propia Historia del hombre que le dan a El protocolo griego todos los ingredientes para ser una novela de éxito.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/23419.Christopher_Priest">About Christopher Priest</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/23419.Christopher_Priest"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1299874167p3/23419.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/23419.Christopher_Priest">Christopher Priest
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
779
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5637663447237944799">Christopher Priest was born in Cheshire, England. He began writing soon after leaving school and has been a full-time freelance writer since 1968.<br/><br/>He has published eleven novels, four short story collections and a number of other books, including critical works, biographies, novelizations and children’s non-fiction.<br/><br/>He has written drama for radio (BBC Radio 4) and television (Thames TV and HTV). In </span>
  <span id="freeText5637663447237944799" style="display:none">Christopher Priest was born in Cheshire, England. He began writing soon after leaving school and has been a full-time freelance writer since 1968.<br /><br />He has published eleven novels, four short story collections and a number of other books, including critical works, biographies, novelizations and children’s non-fiction.<br /><br />He has written drama for radio (BBC Radio 4) and television (Thames TV and HTV). In 2006, The Prestige was made into a major production by Newmarket Films. Directed by Christopher Nolan, The Prestige went straight to No.1 US box office. It received two Academy Award nominations. Other novels, including Fugue For a Darkening Island and The Glamour, are currently in preparation for filming.<br /><br />He is Vice-President of the H. G. Wells Society. In 2007, an exhibition of installation art based on his novel The Affirmation was mounted in London.<br /><br />As a journalist he has written features and reviews for The Times, the Guardian, the Independent, the New Statesman, the Scotsman, and many different magazines.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5637663447237944799" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1688160-the-prestige">Quotes from The Prestige</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Every great magic trick consists of three parts or acts. The first part is called "The Pledge". The magician shows you something ordinary: a deck of cards, a bird or a man. He shows you this object. Perhaps he asks you to inspect it to see if it is indeed real, unaltered, normal. But of course... it probably isn't. The second act is called "The Turn". The magician takes the ordinary something and makes it do something extraordinary. Now you're looking for the secret... but you won't find it, because of course you're not really looking. You don't really want to know. You want to be fooled. But you wouldn't clap yet. Because making something disappear isn't enough; you have to bring it back. That's why every magic trick has a third act, the hardest part, the part we call "The Prestige".&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/91029-every-great-magic-trick-consists-of-three-parts-or-acts">257 likes</a>
            </nobr>

          </div>
